Inheritance After Divorce During Terminal Illness
States the ruling and proof for inheritance after deathbed divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance when a husband divorces during terminal illness, identify the conditions for the wife to inherit, and evaluate the role of intent (tuhma) in the ruling.

Core Idea
A divorcing husband's fraudulent intent is nullified; the wife inherits if he dies during her iddah.

The classical Hanafi position protects the wife from being deprived of inheritance through a deathbed divorce. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof, the conditions for inheritance, and the scope of the husband's fraudulent intent (al-faar). You will learn how the Hanafi school balances legal cause (sabab) and prevention of harm (darar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its legal category
Locate the core ruling: if a husband divorces (revocably or irrevocably) during terminal illness and dies during her iddah, she inherits. If after iddah, she does not. This is a protective measure (wajib) to prevent fraud.
The ruling applies to both revocable (raj'i) and irrevocable (bain) divorce.
2
Analyze the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is based on the marital tie (sabab al-irth) and the husband's intent to nullify it. The law rejects his intent by delaying the divorce's effect until after iddah, preserving inheritance to prevent harm.
This is a case of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking the means to evil) applied via qiyas.
3
Extract the conditions for inheritance
List the conditions from the text: divorce during terminal illness (marad al-mawt), death during iddah, volition (no coercion), illness connected to death (not recovered), and for irrevocable divorce, eligibility throughout.
Coercion (ikrah) nullifies the inheritance according to the preferred view.
4
Examine the sub-rulings and edge cases
The commentary details special cases: when the wife converts or is freed during iddah, when the husband specifies after a general statement, and when the divorce is conditional on emancipation or illness.
These illustrate the operation of the principle of tuhma (suspicion of fraud).
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school does not grant inheritance after a final (bain) divorce even during iddah, while the Hanafi school does. The Hanafi position is based on the continued effect of the marriage tie during iddah for inheritance purposes.
The practical difference (thamarah) is whether the wife inherits or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (طلقها رجعياً أو بائناً في مرض موته ومات في عدتها ورثت وبعدها لا) لأن الزوجية سبب إرثها في مرض موتة والزوج قصد إبطاله فيرد عليه قصده بتأخير عمله إلى زمن انقضاء العدة دفعاً للضرر عنها وقد أمكن لأن النكاح في العدة يبقى في حق بعض الآثار فجاز أن يبقى في حق إرثها عنه بخلاف ما بعد الانقضاء لأنه لا إمكان والزوجية في هذه الحالة ليست بسبب لإرثه عنها فيبطل في حقه خصوصاً إذا رضي به. وفي الظهيرية : وإن كان المطلقة في المرض مستحاضة وكان حيضها مختلفاً ففي الميراث يؤخذ بالأقل لأن المال يستوجب بالشك .اهـ. أطلق الرجعي ليفيد أنها ترث وإن طلق في الصحة ما دامت في العدة لبقاء الزوجية بينهما حقيقة حتى حل الوطء وورثها إذا ماتت فيها، ولا يشترط أهليتها للإرث وقت الطلاق بل وقت موته حتى لو كانت في الرجعي مملوكة أو كتابية ثم أعتقت أو أسلمت في العدة ورثته وأطلق البائن فشمل الواحدة والثلاث وترك المصنف قيد الطواعية ولا بد منه لأنه لو أكره على طلاقها البائن لا ترث كما لو أكرهت على سؤالها الطلاق فإنها لا ترث كما في القنية، وذكر في جامع الفصولين خلافاً فيه. وقيد بأن يكون في مرضه احترازاً عما إذا طلق في الصحة ثم مرض ومات وهي في العدة لا ترث منه، ولو قال صحيح لامرأتيه إحداكما طالق ثم بين في مرضه في إحداهما صار فاراً بالبيان وترث لأنه كالانشاء في حق الإرث للتهمة وتمامه في الكافي وأراد به المرض الذي اتصل به الموت لأن حقها لا يتعلق بماله إلا به فلو طلقها في مرضه ثم صح ثم مات وهي في العدة لا ترث منه كما سيأتي. ولو طلقها في مرضه ثم قتل أو مات من غير ذلك المرض غير أنه لم يبرأ فلها الميراث لأنه قد اتصل الموت بمرضه ؛ كذا في الظهيرية. ولا بد من البائن أن تكون أهلاً للميراث وقت الطلاق والموت وما بينهما وسيأتي ولا يشترط علمه بأهليتها للميراث حتى لو طلقها بائناً في مرضه وقد كان سيدها أعتقها قبل ولم يعلم به الزوج كان فاراً، وكذا لو كان تحته كتابية فأسلمت فطلقها الزوج ثلاثاً وهو لا يعلم بإسلامها كما في الظهيرية، بخلاف ما لو قال المولى لأمته أنت حرة غداً. وقال الزوج أنت طالق ثلاثاً بعد غد إن علم الزوج بكلام المولى كان فاراً وإلا فلا كما في الخانية لأنه وقت التعليق لم يقصد إبطال حقها حيث لم يعلم وإن صارت أهلاً قبل نزول الطلاق ولم تكن حرة وقت التعليق لأن عتقها مضاف بخلاف ما إذا كانت حرة وقته مطلقاً كما في الولوالجية، وصححه في الخانية.
His statement: (If he divorces her revocably or irrevocably during his terminal illness and dies during her waiting period, she inherits from him; if after it, she does not) because the marital tie is the cause of her inheritance from him during his terminal illness, and the husband intended to nullify it, so his intention is rejected by delaying its effect until the end of the waiting period to prevent harm to her, and this is possible because the marriage during the waiting period remains in effect regarding some consequences, so it is permissible for it to remain in effect regarding her inheritance from him, unlike after the expiration because there is no possibility, and the marital tie in this case is not a cause for his inheritance from her, so it becomes void regarding him, especially if he consented to it. End. He mentioned revocable divorce to indicate that she inherits even if he divorces her while healthy as long as she is in the waiting period, because the marital tie between them actually remains, to the extent that intercourse is permissible and he inherits from her if she dies during it. And her eligibility for inheritance at the time of divorce is not required, but rather at the time of his death. So if she was a slave or a kitabiyya in a revocable divorce and then is freed or converts during the waiting period, she inherits from him. And he mentioned irrevocable divorce, which includes one and three (divorces). The author omitted the condition of volition, but it is necessary because if he was coerced into an irrevocable divorce, she does not inherit, just as if she was coerced into asking for divorce, she does not inherit, as in al-Qunyah. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn a difference of opinion is mentioned. He restricted it to being during his illness to exclude the case where he divorces while healthy, then becomes ill and dies during her waiting period; she does not inherit from him. If a healthy man says to his two wives: 'One of you is divorced,' and then during his illness he specifies one of them, he becomes a fugitive (far) by the specification, and she inherits because it is like initiation regarding inheritance due to suspicion, and its completion is in al-Kafi. He meant the illness that is connected to death because her right is only attached to his wealth by it. So if he divorces her during his illness, then recovers, then dies during her waiting period, she does not inherit from him, as will come. If he divorces her during his illness, then is killed or dies from a different illness but he did not recover, she inherits because death was connected to his illness; thus in al-Zahiriyya. For the irrevocable divorce, she must be eligible for inheritance at the time of divorce, death, and the period in between, as will come. His knowledge of her eligibility is not required. So if he divorces her irrevocably during his illness, and her master had freed her before but the husband did not know, he is a fugitive. Similarly, if he had a kitabiyya wife and she converts, and he divorces her three times without knowing of her conversion, as in al-Zahiriyya. Unlike if the master said to his slave-girl: 'You are free tomorrow,' and the husband said: 'You are divorced three times the day after,' if the husband knew of the master's statement, he is a fugitive; otherwise not, as in al-Khaniyya, because at the time of the conditional statement he did not intend to nullify her right since he did not know, and she became eligible before the divorce occurred but was not free at the time of the condition because her emancipation was deferred. Unlike if she was free at that time and he did not know, because it is a legal matter and knowledge is not required. If he made her irrevocable divorce conditional upon her emancipation, he is a fugitive, as in al-Zahiriyya. If he conditioned her divorce upon his illness, such as saying: 'If I become ill, you are divorced three times,' he is a fugitive, because he made the condition of the oath illness in an absolute sense, as in al-Walwaljiyya, and al-Khaniyya deemed it correct.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This is a ruling_with_proof passage. The main ruling: if a husband divorces his wife (whether revocably or irrevocably) during his terminal illness and dies during her waiting period (iddah), she inherits from him. After the iddah expires, she does not inherit. The proof is rational (dalil aqli): the marital tie is the cause of inheritance at the time of terminal illness, and the husband intended to nullify it by divorce, so Islamic law rejects his intention by delaying the effect of the divorce until after the iddah to protect her from harm. This is possible because a revocable divorce does not completely sever marital ties during iddah; certain effects remain, such as allowing inheritance from each other. For irrevocable divorce, although the marital tie is severed, the law still upholds inheritance during iddah to prevent the husband's fraudulent evasion of her rights. The commentary then details various sub-rulings: the wife inherits even if she was a slave or kitabiyya at the time of divorce but becomes free or Muslim during iddah. Coercion (ikrah) prevents inheritance. If the husband was healthy at the time of divorce and later fell ill, she does not inherit. If he specified a wife during illness after a general statement, it is considered a new act (insha') and she inherits. The illness must be connected to death; if he recovers and then dies from another cause, she does not inherit. For irrevocable divorce, she must be eligible to inherit at all times; ignorance of her eligibility does not prevent him from being a fugitive. Conditional divorces linked to emancipation or illness also make him a fugitive.

Key Terms
فار (faar): Literally 'fugitive'; a husband who divorces his wife during terminal illness to deprive her of inheritance; the law treats his divorce as ineffective for inheritance.
خلافاً فيه (khilaafan fihi): There is a difference of opinion on this point within the Hanafi school (regarding coercion and inheritance).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for the wife to inherit when divorced irrevocably (bain) during terminal illness?
A) The husband must not have recovered from his illness before death.
B) The wife must have been Muslim at the time of divorce.
C) The husband must have explicitly intended to deprive her of inheritance.
D) The divorce must have been pronounced in the presence of witnesses.

Key Takeaway
A husband's deathbed divorce does not deprive his wife of inheritance if he dies during her iddah, because the law nullifies his fraudulent intent to protect her rights.

Ignorance of Wife's Eligibility in Inheritance After Irrevocable Divorce
Explains the general rule and exceptional case of deferred emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the husband's ignorance of the wife's eligibility does not exempt him from being a fugitive (faar) in cases of irrevocable divorce during terminal illness, and identify the exceptional case of deferred emancipation where knowledge is relevant.

Core Idea
Ignorance of eligibility does not protect a husband from being a fugitive, except in deferred emancipation.

The Hanafi school strictly enforces the wife's eligibility for inheritance in cases of irrevocable divorce during terminal illness. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the husband's knowledge of her eligibility is generally irrelevant, with a nuanced exception for deferred emancipation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly determining inheritance rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement
The wife must be eligible to inherit (free and Muslim) at three specific times: the moment of divorce, the moment of death, and the period in between. This is a condition for inheritance after an irrevocable divorce during terminal illness.
2
Note the general rule on ignorance
The husband's knowledge of the wife's eligibility is generally not required. Even if he was unaware that she became free or converted, he is still considered a fugitive (faar) and she inherits.
This objective test prevents a husband from using ignorance as a defense.
3
Examine the exception: deferred emancipation
If the wife's emancipation was deferred to a future time (e.g., 'you are free tomorrow'), and the husband divorces her conditionally before that time, his knowledge matters. If he knew of the deferred emancipation, he is a fugitive; if not, he is not.
Rationale: At the time of the conditional divorce, she was not yet free, so his intent to defraud is absent if he did not know.
4
Examine other conditional cases
If the husband makes the divorce conditional on her emancipation (e.g., 'if you become free, you are divorced'), he is a fugitive, because he intended to divorce her once she became eligible. Similarly, if he conditions it on his own illness, he is a fugitive.
In these cases, ignorance of the triggering event is irrelevant because the condition directly targets eligibility.
5
Derive the underlying rationale
The objective fact of eligibility at the relevant times controls, except when the husband's intent is evaluated based on his knowledge at the time of the conditional statement. The exception is limited to deferred emancipation, where the timing of eligibility and the conditional divorce are not simultaneous.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of inheritance after irrevocable divorce during terminal illness. It specifies that the wife must be eligible to inherit (i.e., free and Muslim) at three points: the time of divorce, the time of death, and the period in between. The husband's knowledge of her eligibility is irrelevant. For example, if she was freed before the divorce but the husband did not know, he is still considered a 'faar' (fugitive) and she inherits. Similarly, if a kitabiyya wife converts and the husband divorces her without knowing, she inherits. However, if the emancipation is deferred (e.g., 'you are free tomorrow'), and the husband divorces her conditionally before that, he is only a fugitive if he knew of the master's statement; otherwise not, because at the time of the conditional divorce she was not yet free, so his intent was not to defraud her of inheritance. If the divorce is made conditional on her emancipation, then he is a fugitive because the condition is within his control and he intended to divorce her once she became free. If he makes divorce conditional on his own illness, he is also a fugitive because he intends to divorce her when he falls ill, thereby depriving her of inheritance.

Key Terms
أهلاً للميراث (ahlan lil-miraath): Eligible to inherit; meaning free and Muslim (not a slave or non-Muslim) at the relevant times.
مضاف (mudaaf): Deferred; an emancipation statement that is assigned to a future time, like 'you are free tomorrow.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, because the husband did not intend to affect her inheritance since he did not know.
C) Yes, only if he knew at the time of death.
D) No, because kitabiyya never inherit from Muslim husbands.

Key Takeaway
The husband's ignorance of the wife's eligibility does not prevent her inheritance; only in deferred emancipation does knowledge matter.

Wife’s Direct Initiation of Divorce During Husband’s Terminal Illness
Explains when wife inherits if she causes divorce during husband's fatal illness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a wife loses her inheritance if she directly initiates divorce during her husband's terminal illness, and apply it to scenarios like khul', enabling the son, and conditional divorce.

Core Idea
A wife who directly causes divorce during her husband's fatal illness loses inheritance; the husband's fraud is presumed unless she consented.

This lesson explores the principle of firar (fraud) in the context of divorce during terminal illness. The Hanafi school protects the wife's inheritance from a husband's attempt to disinherit her by divorcing her when he is ill. However, if the wife herself initiates the separation, the presumption of fraud dissolves and she loses her right to inherit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage begins by stating that if the wife directly initiated the reason for divorce (e.g., khul'), she does not inherit. Identify the key phrase 'اختلاعها منه' and understand that this includes any direct action by the wife to cause divorce.
The term 'ikhitila'' encompasses khul' and any direct initiation.
2
Examine the exception for involuntary action
Consider the case where the wife enables the husband's son to kiss her. She inherits only if the son was coerced by his father, because then the action is attributed to the husband, not the wife.
The ruling hinges on consent and volition.
3
Analyze the conditional divorce scenario
Study the case where the husband says 'When I marry you, you are thrice divorced' and marries her during the waiting period. The Hanafi position is that she does not inherit because the divorce was conditioned on her own act of marriage.
Contrary to Muhammad: the text says 'khilafan li-Muhammad'.
4
Distinguish the stranger case
If a third party (not the wife) causes the divorce, the wife inherits because she did not consent. The husband is considered a defrauder (far).
This reinforces that the wife's consent is the decisive factor.
5
Synthesize the principle
Conclude that the wife inherits unless she directly caused the separation or consented to it. Her right to inheritance is nullified only when her own action or explicit consent is present.
Firar (fraud) is presumed in the husband's divorce during illness, but the presumption is rebutted by the wife's initiation.

In al-Kafi: The principle is that those commanded to divorce without compensation each individually can effect the divorce, and those commanded to divorce with compensation cannot individually effect it but rather their meeting is required, and that the grant of authority (tamlik) is confined to the gathering and to agency. No: whoever acts for himself is an owner, and whoever acts for another is an agent. And the wife of a defrauder does not inherit if she directly initiated the cause of separation or its condition, or delayed the two descriptions of the cause or one of the two causes; but if she initiated part of the cause or part of the condition, her right to inheritance is not nullified.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the chapter on divorce of a sick person in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It discusses the inheritance rights of a wife when the husband divorces her during a terminal illness (marad al-mawt) and the wife had a hand in causing the divorce. The key principle is that if the wife directly initiated the cause of separation or consented to it, she does not inherit, because the husband's intent to deprive her of inheritance (firar) is not established. The passage goes through several scenarios: (1) If the wife enables the husband's son to kiss her, she does not inherit unless the son was coerced by his father, because then the action transfers to the father. (2) If separation occurs due to the husband's castration, impotence, or the wife exercising an option, she does not inherit because she consented to the separation. (3) If the wife apostatizes while the husband is ill, she does not inherit. (4) If the husband says, 'When I marry you, you are thrice divorced,' and marries her during her waiting period, she does not inherit because the marriage was her act. (5) If a stranger (not the wife) causes the divorce, the wife inherits because she did not consent. The passage also covers symmetric rules when the wife is terminally ill and initiates divorce: she inherits from him, and he from her, except in cases of divorce due to castration/impotence/li'an where there is a difference of opinion; the relied-upon position (madhhab) is that he does not inherit from her. Rules on apostasy are detailed: if the wife apostatizes during health, husband does not inherit; during illness, he does by istihsan. The principle from al-Kafi is stated about the difference between divorce without compensation (each can act alone) and with compensation (both must agree), and that tamlik (grant of authority) is confined to the gathering. Finally, a summary rule: the wife of a defrauder does not inherit if she directly caused the entire cause or condition, but if only part, her right remains.

Key Terms
الفرار (al-firar): Fraud: the husband's intentional divorce during terminal illness to deprive the wife of inheritance.
الاختلاع (al-ikhitila'): Khul': divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation.
التمليك (al-tamlik): Grant of authority: husband authorizing his wife to divorce herself, confined to the gathering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) She does not inherit because she initiated the divorce.
C) She inherits only if the husband intended fraud.
D) She does not inherit regardless.

Key Takeaway
The wife does not inherit from her terminally ill husband if she directly initiated the divorce or consented to it; the presumption of fraud (firar) is rebutted by her action.

Symmetric Inheritance Rules and Apostasy in Marad al-Mawt
Covers symmetric inheritance rules when wife is sick, apostasy, and general principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that inheritance rights during terminal illness are symmetric between spouses, and identify the rulings on apostasy in the context of marad al-mawt.

Core Idea
Inheritance rights during terminal illness are symmetric: spouses inherit from each other unless the deceased spouse directly caused the separation.

This lesson continues from the previous one, examining the mirror situation where the wife is terminally ill and initiates divorce, as well as the rules when apostasy occurs during illness. The Hanafi school applies similar logic: the healthy spouse inherits from the ill spouse who caused the divorce, but details differ when the divorce is due to defects or li'an.

Guided Path
1
Understand the symmetry principle
The author states that just as the wife's right to her husband's wealth is established during his terminal illness, so is the husband's right to her wealth during her terminal illness. This establishes symmetric treatment.
The basis is that the wealth of the ill person becomes subject to inheritance rules.
2
Analyze the wife's direct initiation while sick
If the wife directly causes separation while she is sick and dies before her waiting period ends, the husband inherits from her. Examples include her exercising options (puberty, emancipation) or kissing her stepson.
These are acts directly from her side that cause the marriage to end.
3
Examine cases of divorce due to defects or li'an
When separation occurs due to castration, impotence, or mutual cursing (li'an) while the wife is sick, there is a difference of opinion. The relied-upon madhhab is that the husband does not inherit from her because the divorce is attributed to him.
Al-Khaniyah and al-Kafi support this view.
4
Study apostasy rulings
If the wife apostatizes during health and dies, the husband does not inherit. If she apostatizes during illness and dies, the husband inherits by istihsan. For the husband's apostasy, the wife inherits regardless of when it occurred.
Istihsan favors the inheritance right of the Muslim spouse when apostasy occurred during illness.
5
Apply the final principle from al-Kafi
The passage ends with a principle about divorce without compensation (each can act alone) vs. with compensation (both must agree), and that tamlik is confined to the gathering. This relates to the distinction between direct and joint initiation.
This supports the earlier differentiation between wife's sole action and joint action.

Consent, and it includes what if separation occurs by enabling the son of the husband, then she does not inherit unless his father ordered him to do that, and he brought her close under compulsion, because by that it transfers to him and he becomes like the direct doer. And it includes what if she left him due to castration or impotence or option of puberty and emancipation, then she does not inherit due to her consent. Similarly, if she apostatized while he is sick. And he indicated by her khul from him that she directly caused the reason for divorce, so it enters into it what if he divorced her irrevocably during his illness then said to her, 'When I marry you, you are thrice divorced,' then he married her during the waiting period and died of his illness, where she does not inherit because it is a death during a future waiting period, so the ruling of fraud is nullified by the first divorce and the second divorce, even if it occurred, except that its condition, which is marriage, occurred by her action, so it is not fraud, contrary to Muhammad; as in al-Khaniyah. And he restricted it to her khul from him because if a stranger freed her from her sick husband with a mortal illness, then she has the inheritance if the husband dies in that illness while she is in the waiting period, because she did not consent to this divorce, so the husband becomes a defrauder; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And the author did not mention the ruling of what if separation occurred from her side during her mortal illness, and it is not hidden that since her right to his wealth is established during his mortal illness, his right to her wealth is established during her mortal illness. So if she directly initiated the cause of separation while she is sick and died before the end of her waiting period, he inherits from her, just as if separation occurred by her own choice in the option of puberty and emancipation, or by her kissing the son of her husband while she is sick, because it is from her side and therefore it was not divorce, and this is apparent. As for if it occurred due to castration or impotence or mutual cursing while she is sick, then the commentator proceeded that it is like the first. In al-Khaniyah, and it is transmitted in Fath al-Qadir from al-Jami', that he does not inherit from her because it is divorce, so it was added to him. And al-Muhit attributed it to al-Jami' as well, confining to it, and al-Kafi decided it, so it is the madhhab. And if the woman apostatized then died or joined the abode of war, if the apostasy was in health, her husband does not inherit from her; if it was during illness, her husband inherits from her by istihsan, contrary to if he apostatized and was killed or joined the abode of war or died on apostasy, then she inherits from him absolutely. And if both apostatized together then one of them embraced Islam then one died, if the Muslim died, the apostate does not inherit; but if the one who died was an apostate and it was the husband, his Muslim wife inherits from him; and if the apostate woman died, if her apostasy was during illness, her Muslim husband inherits from her, if during health, she does not inherit; as in al-Khaniyah. In al-Kafi: The principle is that those commanded to divorce without compensation each individually can effect the divorce, and those commanded to divorce with compensation cannot individually effect it but rather their meeting is required, and that the grant of authority (tamlik) is confined to the gathering and to agency. No: whoever acts for himself is an owner, and whoever acts for another is an agent. And the wife of a defrauder does not inherit if she directly initiated the cause of separation or its condition, or delayed the two descriptions of the cause or one of the two causes; but if she initiated part of the cause or part of the condition, her right to inheritance is not nullified.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the chapter on divorce of a sick person in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It discusses the inheritance rights of a wife when the husband divorces her during a terminal illness (marad al-mawt) and the wife had a hand in causing the divorce. The key principle is that if the wife directly initiated the cause of separation or consented to it, she does not inherit, because the husband's intent to deprive her of inheritance (firar) is not established. The passage goes through several scenarios: (1) If the wife enables the husband's son to kiss her, she does not inherit unless the son was coerced by his father, because then the action transfers to the father. (2) If separation occurs due to the husband's castration, impotence, or the wife exercising an option, she does not inherit because she consented to the separation. (3) If the wife apostatizes while the husband is ill, she does not inherit. (4) If the husband says, 'When I marry you, you are thrice divorced,' and marries her during her waiting period, she does not inherit because the marriage was her act. (5) If a stranger (not the wife) causes the divorce, the wife inherits because she did not consent. The passage also covers symmetric rules when the wife is terminally ill and initiates divorce: she inherits from him, and he from her, except in cases of divorce due to castration/impotence/li'an where there is a difference of opinion; the relied-upon position (madhhab) is that he does not inherit from her. Rules on apostasy are detailed: if the wife apostatizes during health, husband does not inherit; during illness, he does by istihsan. The principle from al-Kafi is stated about the difference between divorce without compensation (each can act alone) and with compensation (both must agree), and that tamlik (grant of authority) is confined to the gathering. Finally, a summary rule: the wife of a defrauder does not inherit if she directly caused the entire cause or condition, but if only part, her right remains.

Key Terms
الفرار (al-firar): Fraud: the husband's intentional divorce during terminal illness to deprive the wife of inheritance.
الاختلاع (al-ikhitila'): Khul': divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation.
الردة (al-riddah): Apostasy: leaving Islam; affects inheritance when it occurs during terminal illness.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; used to allow inheritance when apostasy occurs during illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wife is terminally ill and her husband becomes impotent, leading to separation, and she dies during the waiting period, does the husband inherit?
A) Yes, because the separation is from her side.
B) No, because the divorce is attributed to the husband's defect.
C) Yes, by istihsan.
D) No, because she was sick.

Key Takeaway
Inheritance during terminal illness is symmetric, but when the wife is sick and separation is due to the husband's defects or li'an, the husband does not inherit according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Two Wives and Tamlik: Inheritance Consequences
Complex inheritance rules for tamlik divorce with two wives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a wife inherits from a sick husband when she participates in a tamlik divorce scenario, by applying the Hanafi rule that a wife who directly causes her own divorce loses inheritance.

Core Idea
In tamlik divorce from a sick husband, a wife who directly divorces herself loses inheritance; if divorced by the other, she inherits.

When a terminally ill husband gives his wives the authority to divorce themselves, intricate inheritance questions arise. The Hanafi school examines the precise sequence of each wife's statement to determine who caused her own divorce. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each scenario, teaching you to predict inheritance outcomes based on who acted and when.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's authority-granting statement
Determine whether the husband said "Divorce yourselves" (tamlik) or something else like "if you wish" (conditional) or "the matter is in your hands" (tafwid). This sets the scope of authority.
Tamlik is confined to the gathering; tafwid may allow independent action.
2
Analyze the sequence of wives' statements
Note whether each wife acted on herself, her companion, both, and whether actions were consecutive or simultaneous. The order determines who directly caused each divorce.
3
Apply the rule: a wife who directly divorces herself does not inherit
The key principle: if a wife says "I divorce myself" (either alone or as part of a combined statement), she loses inheritance. If she is divorced only by the other's action, she inherits.
This rule prevents a wife from benefiting by causing her own divorce during the husband's terminal illness.
4
Consider the effect of leaving the gathering
Tamlik is confined to the gathering (majlis). If the wives leave the session before acting, any subsequent divorces are invalid, and they inherit if the husband dies in that illness.
Leaving the gathering voids the authority, so no self-caused divorce occurs.
5
Distinguish tamlik from tafwid and conditional phrasing
"If you wish" requires mutual action; "the matter is in your hands" as tafwid becomes tamlik if intended as delegation, with the nuance that joint action can divorce one of them.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المريض لامرأتيه بعد الدخول طلقا أنفسكما ثلاثاً فطلقت كل نفسها وصاحبتها على التعاقب طلقتا ثلاثاً بتطليق الأولى وتطليق الأخرى نفسها بعد ذلك وصاحبتها باطل فإذا طلقت الأولى نفسها وصاحبتها طلقتا وورثت الثانية دون الأولى بخلاف ما إذا ابتدأت الأولى فطلقت صاحبتها دون نفسها حيث يقع الطلاق على صاحبتها ولم يقع عليها لأنها في حق نفسها مالكة والتمليك يقتصر على المجلس، فإذا بدأت بطلاق صاحبتها خرج الأمر من يدها وورثت، وكذا لو ابتدأت كل واحدة بتطليق صاحبتها لأن كل واحدة طلقت بتطليق غيرها. وإن طلقت كل واحدة نفسها وصاحبتها معا طلقتا ولم يرثا لأن كل واحدة طلقت بتطليق نفسها، وإن طلقت إحداهما بأن قالت إحداهما طلقت نفسي وقالت الأخرى طلقت صاحبتي وخرج الكلامان معاً طلقت تلك الواحدة ولا ترث وإن طلقت إحداهما نفسها ثم طلقتها صاحبتها طلقت ولا ترث وعلى العكس ترث. هذا كله إذا كانتا في مجلسهما ذلك، فإن قامتا عن مجلسهما ذلك ثم . طلقت كل نفسها وصاحبتها معاً أو على التعاقب أو طلقت كل واحدة صاحبتها ورثتا ولو طلقت كل واحدة منهما نفسها لم تطلق واحدة منهما ولو قال طلقا أنفسكما ثلاثاً إن شئتما فطلقت إحداهما نفسها وصاحبتها لم تطلق واحدة منهما حتى تطلق الأخرى نفسها وصاحبتها، فلو طلقت الأخرى بعد ذلك نفسها وصاحبتها ثلاثاً طلقتا وورثت الأولى دون الثانية، لو قامتا المجلس ثم طلقت كل واحدة كليهما متعاقبا أو معاً لا يقع. ولو قال أمركما بأيديكما ناويا التفويض صار تمليكا حتى لا تنفرد إحداهما بالطلاق ويقتصر على المجلس وهو كالتعليق بالمشيئة إلا في حكم واحد وهو أنهما إذا اجتمعا على طلاق واحدة منهما يقع، وفي قوله «إن شئتما لا يقع . ولو قال طلقا انفسكما بألف فقالت كل واحدة طلقت نفسي وصاحبتي بألف معاً أو متعاقباً بانتا بألف ويقسم على مهريهما ولم يرثا، ولو طلقت إحداهما طلقت بحصتها من الألف وإن قامتا من المجلس بطل الأمر . ا هـ مختصراً
A sick man said to his two wives after consummation: 'Divorce yourselves three times.' So each divorced herself and her companion consecutively: they both become thrice divorced by the first one's divorce, and the other's divorce of herself afterwards and her companion is invalid. So if the first divorced herself and her companion, both are divorced and the second inherits but not the first. Contrary to if the first began by divorcing her companion without herself, then the divorce falls on her companion and not on her, because she is owner regarding herself, and the grant of authority (tamlik) is confined to the gathering. So if she began with divorcing her companion, the matter leaves her hand and she inherits. Likewise, if each began by divorcing her companion, because each is divorced by the other's divorce. If each divorced herself and her companion simultaneously, both are divorced and neither inherits, because each is divorced by her own divorce. If one of them said, 'I divorced myself' and the other said, 'I divorced my companion,' and both statements came together, that one (the one who said 'I divorced myself') is divorced and does not inherit. If one divorced herself then her companion divorced her, she is divorced and does not inherit; and in the opposite case she inherits. All this if they were in that gathering. If they left that gathering, then each divorced herself and her companion simultaneously or consecutively, or each divorced her companion, they both inherit. If each divorced herself, none is divorced. If he said, 'Divorce yourselves three times if you wish,' and one of them divorced herself and her companion, neither is divorced until the other also divorces herself and her companion. So if the other afterwards divorced herself and her companion three times, both are divorced and the first inherits but not the second. If they left the gathering, then each divorced both, consecutively or simultaneously, it does not occur. If he said, 'The matter is in your hands,' intending delegation (tafwid), it becomes a tamlik, so that neither can act alone, and it is confined to the gathering, and it is like a conditional upon will except in one ruling: that if they both agree on divorcing one of them, it occurs; whereas in his saying 'if you wish' it does not occur. If he said, 'Divorce yourselves for a thousand,' and each said, 'I divorced myself and my companion for a thousand,' simultaneously or consecutively, they become irrevocably divorced for a thousand, which is divided between their mahr, and they do not inherit. If only one divorced, she is divorced for her share of the thousand, and if they leave the gathering, the matter becomes void. End. Abridged.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a detailed kasuistic analysis of a specific scenario in the chapter on divorce of a sick person: a terminally ill husband, after consummation, tells his two wives, 'Divorce yourselves three times' (طلقا أنفسكما ثلاثاً). The question is: who inherits? The answer depends on the order and timing of the wives' statements. The husband's statement is a tamlik (grant of authority), which is confined to the gathering (المجلس). Each wife can only act within that gathering, and once she acts, her authority ends. The key rule: if a wife divorces herself (طلاق نفسها), she loses inheritance because she directly caused the divorce (and thus is not a victim of fraud). If a wife is divorced by the other (طلاق صاحبتها), she inherits because she did not act. The passage examines various sequences: consecutive, simultaneous, with or without conditions ('if you wish'), and with compensation (asking for a thousand). It also distinguishes between tamlik and tafwid (delegation). The principle from al-Kafi about tamlik being confined to the gathering is applied. The purpose is to show how the inheritance rule (wife does not inherit if she directly causes divorce) is applied in multi-wife scenarios, with intricate results.

Key Terms
التمليك (al-tamlik): Grant of authority: husband authorizing wife to divorce herself; confined to the gathering.
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation: husband delegating the right of divorce to the wife; similar to tamlik but with nuance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The divorce is invalid because they acted simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
A wife who directly divorces herself in a tamlik scenario loses inheritance; a wife divorced by the other retains it.

Wife Asking for Revocable Divorce but Receiving Triple Bain: Inheritance Ruling
Ruling on inheritance when wife asks for revocable divorce but receives triple bain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling and legal reasoning when a wife asks for a revocable divorce but receives a triple bain (irrevocable) divorce, and apply the principle of istihsan to determine inheritance entitlement.

Core Idea
A wife who asks for revocable divorce does not forfeit inheritance if the husband gives irrevocably; he becomes the initiator.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a subtle scenario: a wife in her husband's terminal illness asks for a revocable divorce, but the husband divorces her with three (or a single) irrevocable divorce. The key question is whether the wife still inherits. The author navigates the interplay between the wife's request and the husband's action, establishing that the wife retains her inheritance rights because she did not consent to the irreversible break. The ruling is grounded in Hanafi principles of istihsan and the default classification of an unspecified request as revocable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The wife asks for a revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī); the husband gives a triple divorce (ṭalāq bāʾin). The ruling: she inherits from him if he dies during her ʿiddah.
The same ruling applies if he gives a single bāʾin divorce.
2
Locate the dalīl (evidence)
The author cites established Hanafi principles: a revocable divorce does not terminate the marriage; the default when a wife says 'divorce me' is a revocable divorce. This is supported by references to al-Khāniyyah, al-Muḥīṭ, and Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn.
No Quran or Hadith is directly quoted; the proof is based on analogy (qiyās) and juristic preference (istiḥsān).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah (legal link)
The wife's request is for a revocable divorce, which does not end the marriage permanently. Therefore, she did not consent to the loss of her inheritance rights. The husband, by giving more than requested, becomes the initiator of the bāʾin divorce, so his action does not nullify her inheritance.
The principle: 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā' (matters are by their intentions) is implicit here.
4
Note the scope and subcases
The author extends the ruling to three scenarios: (1) wife says 'divorce me revocably' and he gives three; (2) wife says 'divorce me' (unspecified) and he gives a bāʾin; (3) wife says 'divorce me' and he gives three. The author notes uncertainty about a fourth subcase (wife asks for one bāʾin, he gives three), but al-Muḥīṭ suggests she inherits by istiḥsān.
This shows the fiqh method of expanding the ruling through taʿlīl (reasoning).
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ (preferred view)
The muftā bihī (operative ruling) is that she inherits in all cases where she asked for less than the husband gave. This is based on the principle that the husband's excessive action makes him the initiator, and istiḥsān overrides any potential qiyās that might deny inheritance.
The authority weight is from the later fatwā literature (fatawā mutaʾakhkhirīn), relying on al-Muḥīṭ and Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفي طلقني رجعية فطلقها ثلاثاً ورثت) لما قدمنا أن الرجعي لا يزيل النكاح فلم تكن بسؤالها راضية ببطلان حقها، وأراد من ذكر الرجعية نفي سؤالها البائن فدخل ما لو قالت طلقني ولم تزد عليه فطلقها بائناً فإنها ترث لأنه ينصرف إلى الرجعي عند الاطلاق كما في الخانية، وكذا ينصرف إليه في الوكالة والتفويض والإنشاء فلم تكن بسؤالها راضية ببطلان حقها. والمراد بالثلاث البائن فدخل ما لو طلقها واحدة بائنة أيضاً ولم أر حكم ما إذا سألته واحدة بائنة فطلقها ثلاثاً وظاهر المحيط أنها ترث فإنه قال : لو قالت له طلقني فطلقها ثلاثاً ورثت استحساناً لأنها سألته في الواحدة قوله : (فدخل ما لو قالت طلقني ولم تزد عليه الخ) قال في جامع الفصولين : قالت له في مرضه طلقني فطلقها ثلاثاً فمات في العدة ترثه إذ صار مبتدئاً فلا يبطل حقها في الإرث كقولها
His statement: (And in 'Divorce me revocably' and he divorced her three times, she inherits) because we have stated that a revocable divorce does not remove the marriage, so by her asking she was not consenting to the nullification of her right. And he intended by mentioning 'revocable' to negate her asking for an irrevocable divorce. So it includes what if she said 'Divorce me' without adding anything, and he divorced her irrevocably, then she inherits, because it is understood as revocable when absolute, as in al-Khaniyah. Similarly, it is understood as revocable in agency, delegation, and creation, so by her asking she was not consenting to the nullification of her right. And what is meant by 'three' is the irrevocable (bain). So it includes what if he divorced her with a single irrevocable divorce as well. And I have not seen the ruling of what if she asked for a single irrevocable divorce and he divorced her three times; and the apparent meaning of al-Muhit is that she inherits, for he said: If she said to him 'Divorce me' and he divorced her three times, she inherits by istihsan, because she asked him for one [revocable?]. His statement: (So it includes what if she said 'Divorce me' without adding etc.) He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: She said to him during his illness 'Divorce me', so he divorced her three times, then he died during her waiting period, she inherits from him, since he became an initiator, so her right to inheritance is not nullified, like her saying...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq). It addresses a specific scenario: a wife asks her terminally ill husband for a revocable divorce (طلقة رجعية), but the husband gives her a triple divorce (three divorces), which is an irrevocable (bain) divorce. The ruling: she inherits from him if he dies during her waiting period. The reasoning is that by asking for a revocable divorce, she did not intend to deprive herself of inheritance, because a revocable divorce does not end the marriage immediately; the wife remains in the marriage during the waiting period. The husband, by giving more than she asked (triple), is considered the initiator of the bain divorce, so he is the one committing fraud (firar). The passage extends the same rule if she simply said 'Divorce me' without specifying, because the default understanding is a revocable divorce. It also notes that the same applies if he gave a single irrevocable divorce (bain) instead of three. The passage cites al-Khaniyah, al-Muhit, and Jami' al-Fusulayn. If she explicitly asked for a single bain divorce and he gave three, the apparent ruling from al-Muhit is that she inherits (by istihsan) because she did not ask for three. The text ends with a quote from Jami' al-Fusulayn emphasizing that the husband becomes the initiator of the triple divorce and thus the wife's inheritance right remains intact.

Key Terms
الرجعية (al-raj'iyyah): Revocable divorce: divorce that does not end the marriage immediately; husband can take wife back without new contract.
البائن (al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce: divorce that ends the marriage immediately; husband cannot take wife back without new marriage.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference: deviation from analogical reasoning in favor of a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
During her husband's terminal illness, a wife asks for a revocable divorce. The husband divorces her with a single irrevocable (bain) divorce. Does she inherit if he dies during her waiting period?
A) No, because she asked for divorce.
B) Yes, because she asked for revocable, not bain.
C) No, because a single bain is still a divorce.
D) Yes, but only if he dies before the divorce is executed.

Key Takeaway
A wife retains inheritance when her request for revocable divorce is exceeded by the husband's irrevocable divorce, because she did not consent to the permanent termination of marriage rights.

Tamlik to Two Wives: Sequence, Session, and Inheritance
Explains rulings on tamlik to two wives with various sequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for each scenario of delegated divorce (tamlik) to two wives and determine inheritance based on the sequence of their actions.

Core Idea
A wife's delegation expires when she leaves the session; divorcing herself forfeits inheritance, but divorcing her co-wife preserves it.

When a sick husband delegates divorce to his two wives, the legal consequences depend on the sequence and simultaneity of their actions. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks multiple scenarios to show how the principle of session confinement (al-majlis) and the wife's ownership over her own divorce determine both the validity of the divorce and the right to inherit.

Guided Path
1
Understand tamlik and session confinement
Tamlik gives each wife the right to divorce herself or her co-wife, but only within the same session (al-majlis). This principle is the foundation for all scenarios.
If the wives leave the session, their right to act is lost.
2
Analyze sequential actions
Consider the scenario where each wife divorces herself and her co-wife successively. The first wife's action is effective; the second wife's action after the first is invalid because she can only act while still in session and having not already been divorced.
3
Determine inheritance effects
If a wife divorces herself, she forfeits inheritance because the marriage ends by her own act. If she only divorces her co-wife (without divorcing herself), she retains inheritance rights as a widow.
Exception: when both act simultaneously, neither inherits.
4
Handle conditional language and monetary consideration
If the husband uses conditional phrases ('if you wish') or offers money ('for a thousand'), additional rules apply. For conditional, both must agree; for monetary, the division depends on dower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنفسكما ثلاثاً فطلقت كل نفسها وصاحبتها على التعاقب طلقتا ثلاثاً بتطليق الأولى وتطليق الأخرى نفسها بعد ذلك وصاحبتها باطل فإذا طلقت الأولى نفسها وصاحبتها طلقتا وورثت الثانية دون الأولى بخلاف ما إذا ابتدأت الأولى فطلقت صاحبتها دون نفسها حيث يقع الطلاق على صاحبتها ولم يقع عليها لأنها في حق نفسها مالكة والتمليك يقتصر على المجلس، فإذا بدأت بطلاق صاحبتها خرج الأمر من يدها وورثت، وكذا لو ابتدأت كل واحدة بتطليق صاحبتها لأن كل واحدة طلقت بتطليق غيرها. وإن طلقت كل واحدة نفسها وصاحبتها معا طلقتا ولم يرثا لأن كل واحدة طلقت بتطليق نفسها، وإن طلقت إحداهما بأن قالت إحداهما طلقت نفسي وقالت الأخرى طلقت صاحبتي وخرج الكلامان معاً طلقت تلك الواحدة ولا ترث وإن طلقت إحداهما نفسها ثم طلقتها صاحبتها طلقت ولا ترث وعلى العكس ترث. هذا كله إذا كانتا في مجلسهما ذلك، فإن قامتا عن مجلسهما ذلك ثم طلقت كل نفسها وصاحبتها معاً أو على التعاقب أو طلقت كل واحدة صاحبتها ورثتا ولو طلقت كل واحدة منهما نفسها لم تطلق واحدة منهما ولو قال طلقا أنفسكما ثلاثاً إن شئتما فطلقت إحداهما نفسها وصاحبتها لم تطلق واحدة منهما حتى تطلق الأخرى نفسها وصاحبتها، فلو طلقت الأخرى بعد ذلك نفسها وصاحبتها ثلاثاً طلقتا وورثت الأولى دون الثانية، لو قامتا المجلس ثم طلقت كل واحدة كليهما متعاقبا أو معاً لا يقع. ولو قال أمركما بأيديكما ناويا التفويض صار تمليكا حتى لا تنفرد إحداهما بالطلاق ويقتصر على المجلس وهو كالتعليق بالمشيئة إلا في حكم واحد وهو أنهما إذا اجتمعا على طلاق واحدة منهما يقع، وفي قوله «إن شئتما لا يقع . ولو قال طلقا أنفسكما بألف فقالت كل واحدة طلقت نفسي وصاحبتي بألف معاً أو متعاقباً بانتا بألف ويقسم على مهريهما ولم يرثا، ولو طلقت إحداهما طلقت بحصتها من الألف وإن قامتا من المجلس بطل الأمر . اهـ مختصراً
You both divorce yourselves three times, and each divorced herself and her co-wife successively: they are divorced three times by the divorce of the first, and the divorce of the other wife for herself afterwards and for her co-wife is invalid. When the first divorced herself and her co-wife, both are divorced and the second inherits, not the first, unlike if the first began and divorced her co-wife without herself: the divorce falls on her co-wife and not on her because she is owner over herself and delegation is confined to the session. If she began with divorcing her co-wife, the matter left her hand and she inherits. Similarly if each began by divorcing her co-wife, because each is divorced by another's divorce. If each divorced herself and her co-wife simultaneously, both are divorced and do not inherit because each is divorced by her own divorce. If one of them said 'I divorced myself' and the other said 'I divorced my co-wife' and the two statements came together, that one is divorced and does not inherit. If one divorced herself then her co-wife divorced her, she is divorced and does not inherit, and in the opposite case she inherits. All this if they are in that same session. If they leave that session then each divorces herself and her co-wife together or successively, or each divorces her co-wife, they inherit. If each divorced herself, neither is divorced. If he said 'Divorce yourselves three times if you wish', and one divorced herself and her co-wife, neither is divorced until the other divorces herself and her co-wife. If the other thereafter divorces herself and her co-wife three times, both are divorced and the first inherits, not the second. If they leave the session then each divorces both successively or together, it does not occur. If he said 'The matter is in your hands' intending delegation, it becomes tamlik such that one cannot act alone and it is confined to the session, and it is like conditional on will except in one ruling: if they agree on divorcing one of them, it occurs, whereas in 'if you wish' it does not. If he said 'Divorce yourselves for a thousand', and each said 'I divorced myself and my co-wife for a thousand' together or successively, they become separated for a thousand divided according to their dowers, and they do not inherit. If one divorced, she is divorced for her share of the thousand. If they leave the session, the matter is void. End summary.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq details the rulings when a sick husband delegates divorce to two wives. The delegation (tamlik) gives each wife the right to divorce herself or her co-wife, but the authority is confined to the session. The author presents multiple scenarios: sequential actions, simultaneous actions, and the effect of leaving the session. The key principle is that a wife can only act in her own capacity; if she divorces herself, she loses inheritance rights because she terminated the marriage. If she only divorces her co-wife, she preserves her right to inherit. The commentary explains the legal reasoning based on ownership and session confinement, and also covers cases with conditional language ('in shi'tuma') and with a monetary consideration.

Key Terms
التمليك (al-tamlik): Delegation of the right to divorce by the husband to the wife, where she becomes owner of the right to effect divorce.
المجلس (al-majlis): The session in which the delegation is made; the right to divorce is confined to that session.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a sick husband says to two wives 'Divorce yourselves three times', and each divorces herself and her co-wife simultaneously, what is the inheritance ruling?
A) Both wives inherit
B) Neither wife inherits
C) Only the first wife inherits
D) Only the second wife inherits

Key Takeaway
The validity of tamlik and inheritance rights hinge on the session confinement and whether the wife acted on her own behalf or on behalf of her co-wife.

Inheritance When Wife Requests a Raj'i Divorce but Gets Three
Ruling with proof on wife's inheritance after divorce request

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a wife's inheritance when she asks for a raj'i divorce and receives three, and explain the reasoning using the principle of default interpretation (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad).

Core Idea
A wife who requests a raj'i divorce does not consent to losing her inheritance, even if the husband gives a bain divorce.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a subtle scenario: a wife asks her husband for a raj'i (revocable) divorce, but the husband instead gives three divorces (which are bain, irrevocable). Does she still inherit? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on the principle that her request is interpreted as raj'i by default, and she never consented to a bain divorce that would sever inheritance rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's request
Determine what the wife asked for. If she explicitly said 'raj'i' or simply 'divorce me', the Hanafi default is that it is a request for a raj'i (revocable) divorce.
This is based on the rule that the absolute is carried to the raj'i, as in al-Khaniyya.
2
Classify the husband's response
The husband gave three divorces, which are bain (irrevocable). This is more than what she requested.
Three divorces immediately dissolve the marriage, but the key is the wife's intent.
3
Apply the default interpretation principle
Since her request is interpreted as raj'i, she did not consent to a bain divorce. Therefore, she did not agree to forfeit her inheritance.
The ruling is by istihsan (juristic preference) as stated in al-Muhit.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars argue that if she explicitly asked for bain, she would not inherit. But the mufta bihi is that she inherits because the husband became the initiator by giving more than requested.
This ikhtilaf is noted in Jami' al-Fusulayn.
5
State the practical ruling
In all cases where the wife requested raj'i or unspecified divorce, she inherits even if the husband gives a bain divorce. Her inheritance is not forfeited unless she explicitly consented to bain.
The wife's right is protected because she did not voluntarily relinquish it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفي طلقني رجعية فطلقها ثلاثاً ورثت) لما قدمنا أن الرجعي لا يزيل النكاح فلم تكن بسؤالها راضية ببطلان حقها، وأراد من ذكر الرجعية نفي سؤالها البائن فدخل ما لو قالت طلقني ولم تزد عليه فطلقها بائناً فإنها ترث لأنه ينصرف إلى الرجعي عند الاطلاق كما في الخانية، وكذا ينصرف إليه في الوكالة والتفويض والإنشاء فلم تكن بسؤالها راضية ببطلان حقها. والمراد بالثلاث البائن فدخل ما لو طلقها واحدة بائنة أيضاً ولم أر حكم ما إذا سألته واحدة بائنة فطلقها ثلاثاً وظاهر المحيط أنها ترث فإنه قال : لو قالت له طلقني فطلقها ثلاثاً ورثت استحساناً لأنها سألته في الواحدة قوله : (فدخل ما لو قالت طلقني ولم تزيد عليه الخ) قال في جامع الفصولين : قالت له في مرضه طلقني فطلقها ثلاثاً فمات في العدة ترثه إذ صار مبتدئاً فلا يبطل حقها في الإرث كقولها ثلاثاً ورثت وإن أبانها بأمرها في مرضه أو تصادقا عليها في الصحة ومضى العدة فاقرأ وقد طلقها ثلاثاً .انتهى. ولم يعلل بالرجعي وإنما علل بالواحدة وينبغي أن لا ميراث لها لرضاها بالبائن .
His statement: (And in 'Divorce me raj'i, and he divorced her three times, she inherits) because we said that raj'i does not remove the marriage, so she was not by her request consenting to the annulment of her right. And he intended by mentioning raj'i to negate her request for bain, so it includes if she said 'Divorce me' without adding, and he divorced her bain: she inherits because it is interpreted as raj'i in absolute terms as in al-Khaniyya. Similarly it is interpreted in agency, delegation, and creation, so she was not by her request consenting to the annulment of her right. And the intended by 'three' is bain, so it includes if he divorced her one bain also. And I have not seen the ruling if she asked for one bain and he divorced her three, and apparently from al-Muhit that she inherits, for he said: If she said 'Divorce me' and he divorced her three, she inherits by istihsan because she asked for one. His statement: (So it includes if she said 'Divorce me' and did not add etc.) He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: She said to him in his illness 'Divorce me' and he divorced her three, and he died in the 'idda, she inherits him because he became an initiator, so her right to inheritance is not void. Like her saying 'three' and she inherits, and if he separated her by her command in his illness or they both agreed on it in health and the 'idda passed, then read... and he divorced her three. End. And he did not give the reason as raj'i but rather as one, and it should be that she has no inheritance due to her consent to bain.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage comments on a ruling in Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding a wife who asks for a raj'i (revocable) divorce but the husband gives three divorces (which are bain, irrevocable). The commentator explains that since raj'i divorce does not dissolve the marriage immediately, the wife by asking for it did not consent to losing her inheritance rights. Therefore, she inherits. The same applies if she simply says 'Divorce me' without specifying, as it defaults to raj'i. Even if she asks for one bain divorce and he gives three, according to al-Muhit she inherits because she only asked for one. However, the commentator notes a difference of opinion: in Jami' al-Fusulayn, the reason given is that the husband became an initiator (mubtadi') by giving more than requested. The commentator leans towards no inheritance if she consented to bain, but acknowledges the other view.

Key Terms
الرجعي (al-raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage contract.
البائن (al-ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that immediately dissolves the marriage, ending inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wife says to her husband 'Divorce me' without specifying, and he divorces her three times, does she inherit according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) Yes, because the request defaults to raj'i divorce
B) No, because she asked for divorce
C) Yes, only if he dies within the 'idda
D) No, because three divorces are bain

Key Takeaway
A wife who requests a raj'i or unspecified divorce retains inheritance rights even if the husband gives a bain divorce, because she did not consent to the bain divorce.

Tuhma in Iqrar/Wasiyya After Divorce by Wife's Request
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tuhma after divorce by wife's request

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on tuhma when a wife is divorced at her request in illness and analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Tuhma persists even after 'idda according to Abu Hanifa, limiting the wife to the lesser of iqrar/wasiyya and inheritance.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of tuhma (suspicion of collusion) when a husband divorces his wife at her request during his illness and subsequently makes an iqrar (acknowledgment) or wasiyya (bequest) in her favor. The core question is whether the tuhma that arises from the divorce-separation extends beyond the 'idda period, affecting the validity of later financial arrangements.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the ruling as given by Imam Abu Hanifa: the wife receives the lesser of the iqrar/wasiyya amount and her inheritance share from the husband. This is due to the persistence of tuhma.
Tuhma here refers to the possibility that the divorce was a collusive arrangement to later funnel extra wealth to the wife, depriving other heirs.
2
Identify the opposing view of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that once the 'idda ends, the wife becomes a legal stranger (ajnabiyya), so no tuhma applies. Hence, she is entitled to the full iqrar/wasiyya.
They base this on practical consequences: she can receive zakat, testify for him, and marry another man.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's evidence
Their evidence is the permissibility of giving zakat to her, accepting his testimony for her, and her marriage to another. These indicate she is no longer under marital influence.
These actions are normally prohibited during 'idda or between spouses.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa argues that no customary collusion (muwada'a) exists in matters of zakat, testimony, or marriage. Those are exceptional acts and do not negate the tuhma specific to financial transfers like iqrar and wasiyya.
The tuhma targets the increase of her share through the very mechanism of divorce itself.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, the wife gets less (the lesser of the two amounts), protecting the interests of other heirs. Under the Sahibayn, she gets the full iqrar/wasiyya, which could be larger than her inheritance.
The dispute illustrates how jurists weigh potential collusion against formal legal status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن أبانها بأمرها في مرضه أو تصادقا عليها في الصحة ومضى العدة فاقر أو أوصى لها فلها الأقل منها ومن ارثها أي لها الأقل من كل واحد من المقر به والموصى به ومن إرثها منه لأن العدة باقية في المسألة الأولى وهي سبب التهمة والحكم يدار على دليل التهمة، وفي الثانية قال الإمام ببقاء التهمة أيضاً لأن المرأة قد تختار الطلاق لينفتح باب الإقرار والوصية فيزيد حقها والزوجان قد يتواضعان على الإقرار بالفرقة وانقضاء العدة وهذه التهمة في الزيادة فرددناها ولا تهمة في قدر الميراث فصححناه وهما قالا في الثانية بنفي التهمة لكونها أجنبية لعدم العدة بدليل قبول شهادته لها وجواز وضع الزكاة فيها وتزوجها بزوج آخر. وأجاب الإمام الأعظم رضي الله عنه بأنه لا مواضعة عادة في حق الزكاة والشهادة والتزوج فلا تهمة. هذا حاصل ما في الهداية وقرره الشارحون من غير تعقب وهو ظاهر في أنه إذا أقر بالطلاق منذ زمان وصدقته أن العدة تعتبر من وقت الطلاق بدليل أنهم اتفقوا هنا أنه يجوز له دفع الزكاة إليها وشهادته لها وتزوجها وهو خلاف ما صرحوا به في العدة أن من الفتوى على أن العدة تعتبر من وقت الإقرار كما في الهداية والخانية وغيرهما، فلا يثبت شيء من هذه الأحكام ولا تزوجه بأختها وأربع سواها أيضاً فحينئذ ظهرت التهمة في إقراره أنه ووصيته واندفع به ما ذكره السروجي في غايته من ينبغي تحكيم الحال فإن كان جرى طلقني رجعياً فأبانها ا هـ قوله : ( وينبغي أن لا ميراث لها لرضاها بالبائن هذا هو الظاهر وهو مقتضى إطلاق المصنف بقوله سابقاً وإن أبانها بأمرها كما أشار إليه في النهر لكن ما في جامع الفصولين المذكور آنفاً يفيد أنها ترث لأنه علل بقوله إذ صار مبتدئاً أي أوقع شيئاً لم تطلبه فكأنه مسألة أوقع الثلاث ابتداء بدون طلب تأمل قوله : واندفع به ما ذكره السروجي الخ) أي آخذاً . الطلاق الآتية قريباً عن الذخيرة كما في النهر قوله : (وقد رده في فتح القدير بوجه آخر الخ) قال في النهر : وأنت خبير بأن اعتزالها عنه في مرضه الذي هو زمان للرحمة والشفقة ظاهر أيضاً في خصومته. والإيصاء لها بالأكثر قد يكون طمعاً في ابراء ذمته وتذكيراً بسبق مودته، وقد قرر في العدة عند قول صاحب الهداية ومشايخنا يعني مشايخ بخاری سمرقند يفتون في الطلاق أن ابتداءها من وقت الإقرار نفياً للتهمة والمواضعة ا.هـ. يعني فلا يصح إقرار المريض لها بالدين أو ليتزوج أختها أو أربعاً سواها وإذا كان مخالفة هذا الحكم بهذه التهمة فينبغي أن يتحرى به محال التهمة والناس الذين هم مظانها ولذا فصل السعدي حيث قال : ما ذكر محمد من ابتدائها من وقت الطلاق محمول على ما إذا كانا متفرقين من الوقت الذي أسند الطلاق إليه، أما إذا كانا مجتمعين فالكذب في كلامه ظاهر فلا يصدقان في الاسناد ا.هـ. وهذا كما ترى ظاهر في تحكيم الحال وإذا ثبت التهمة وكان ابتداؤها من
His statement: (And if he separated her by her command in his illness or they both agreed on it in health and the 'idda passed, and he made an iqrar or wasiyya for her, then she has the less of iqrar/wasiyya and her inheritance) meaning she has the less of each of the iqrar and wasiyya and of her inheritance from him, because the 'idda remains in the first case and it is a cause of tuhma, and the ruling is based on the evidence of tuhma. In the second case, Imam [Abu Hanifa] said tuhma remains because the woman may choose divorce to open the door of iqrar and wasiyya to increase her right, and the couple may collude in iqrar of separation and end of 'idda. This tuhma is related to increase, so we reject it, and there is no tuhma in the amount of inheritance so we affirm it. The two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said in the second case: no tuhma because she is a stranger due to no 'idda, evidenced by the permissibility of his testimony for her, the permissibility of giving zakat to her, and her marrying another husband. The Greatest Imam answered that there is no customary collusion regarding zakat, testimony, and marriage, so no tuhma. This is the gist of what is in al-Hidaya and the commentators confirmed without critique. And it is apparent that if he acknowledged the divorce from a past time and she confirmed him, the 'idda is considered from the time of divorce, as evidenced by their agreement here that it is permissible for him to give zakat to her, his testimony for her, and marry her, which contradicts what they stated in 'idda that the fatwa is that 'idda is from the time of iqrar, as in al-Hidaya, al-Khaniyya, etc. So none of these rulings are established, nor his marriage to her sister or four others. Then the tuhma appears in his iqrar and wasiyya, and what al-Saruji mentioned in his Ghaya is rebutted: that the situation should be judged; if it occurred that she said 'Divorce me raj'i' and he separated her... etc. His statement: (And it should be that she has no inheritance due to her consent to bain; this is apparent and is the implication of the author's earlier statement 'and if he separated her by her command' as indicated in al-Nahr, but what is in Jami' al-Fusulayn mentioned above indicates that she inherits because he gave the reason 'since he became an initiator', meaning he did something she did not ask for, so it is like the case where he initiated three without request. Ponder.) His statement: And rebutted by what al-Saruji mentioned etc. i.e., taking... The divorces to come shortly from al-Dhakhira as in al-Nahr. His statement: (And it was rebutted in Fath al-Qadir with another aspect etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And you are an expert that her seclusion from him in his illness, which is a time of mercy and compassion, is also apparent in animosity. And bequeathing to her more may be greed to clear his conscience and a reminder of past affection. And it was established in 'idda regarding the statement of the author of al-Hidaya and our shaykhs, meaning the shaykhs of Bukhara and Samarkand, that they give fatwa in divorce that its beginning is from the time of iqrar to negate tuhma and collusion. i.e., so the iqrar of the sick person for her for debt or to marry her sister or four others is not valid. And since this ruling contradicts this tuhma, one should investigate places of tuhma and people who are likely to be subject to it. Hence al-Sa'di distinguished saying: What Muhammad mentioned about its beginning from the time of divorce is carried on the case when they were separated from the time to which the divorce was attributed; but if they were together, the lie in his statement is apparent, so they are not believed in the attribution. This, as you see, is apparent in judging the situation. And when tuhma is established and its beginning is from...
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the issue of tuhma (suspicion) when a wife is divorced at her request during the husband's illness, and then the husband subsequently makes an iqrar (acknowledgement) or wasiyya (bequest) in her favor. The ruling is that she receives the lesser of the iqrar/wasiyya and her inheritance. Abu Hanifa maintains that tuhma persists even after the 'idda, because the wife may have chosen divorce to enable the husband to give her more through iqrar/wasiyya. The two disciples argue that once the 'idda ends, she is a stranger, so there is no tuhma, as evidenced by the permissibility of giving her zakat etc. The commentator also discusses the related issue of whether the 'idda starts from the time of actual divorce or from the time of iqrar, noting a difference between earlier and later fatwas. The passage ends with a discussion of the need to consider the specific circumstances (tahkim al-hal).

Key Terms
التهمة (al-tuhma): Suspicion of collusion between spouses to deprive heirs by the husband's iqrar or wasiyya after divorce.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or debt, here by the sick husband in favor of his divorced wife.
الوصية (al-wasiyya): Bequest made by a person during illness, here in favor of a divorced wife after 'idda.
العدة (al-'idda): Waiting period after divorce before a woman can remarry; its passing makes her a stranger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a wife is divorced by her own request during her husband's illness, and after the 'idda passes the husband makes a will (wasiyya) for her, what does she receive?
A) The full amount of the wasiyya
B) Nothing, because she is a stranger
C) The lesser of the wasiyya and her inheritance share
D) Her full inheritance share only

Key Takeaway
The persistence of tuhma after 'idda in divorce-by-wife's-request cases is a matter of ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa (who retains tuhma) and the Sahibayn (who end it), affecting whether the wife receives the full iqrar/wasiyya or only the lesser of that and her inheritance.

Inheritance of a Woman Divorced at Her Request During Terminal Illness
Intra-Hanafi dispute on suspicion after iddah in terminal illness divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the persistence of suspicion (tuhma) after iddah in cases of divorce at the wife's request during terminal illness, and apply the ruling that she takes the lesser of the acknowledged/bequeathed amount and her inheritance.

Core Idea
Suspicion of collusion persists even after iddah per Abu Hanifa, restricting the wife's inheritance unless circumstances prove otherwise.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq delves into a nuanced scenario: a woman who requests divorce during her husband's terminal illness. The central issue is whether the wife can inherit after the divorce, especially when the husband acknowledges or bequeaths something to her. The Hanafi school's internal disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hinges on the persistence of suspicion (tuhma) of collusion. Understanding this dispute will sharpen your ability to apply the principle that legal rulings are driven by evidence of collusion, not mere formalities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
First, understand the scenario: a husband in terminal illness divorces his wife at her request (or they mutually agree on divorce in health) and later the husband acknowledges or bequeaths something to her after the iddah has passed. The initial ruling from the matn is that she takes the lesser of the acknowledged/bequeathed amount and her inheritance share.
The matn distinguishes between revocable (one) and irrevocable (three) divorce; the ruling applies to revocable because she consented to irrevocable forfeits inheritance.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The dispute is about whether suspicion (tuhma) persists after the iddah in the case of mutual agreement in health. Abu Hanifa holds that suspicion remains; the Sahibayn hold that after iddah she is a stranger and no suspicion exists.
The disagreement is recorded in Al-Hidayah and the commentators affirm it.
3
Examine each position's proof
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof: the iddah is the cause of suspicion, and the ruling revolves around evidence of suspicion; there is no customary collusion in zakat, testimony, or marriage. Then the Sahibayn's proof: after iddah she is a stranger, evidenced by permissible zakat, testimony, and marriage; these would be prohibited if suspicion persisted.
Note that the Sahibayn use the permissibility of these actions as evidence of no suspicion.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: if suspicion persists (Abu Hanifa), she gets the lesser of the acknowledged/bequeathed amount and her inheritance; if no suspicion (Sahibayn), she inherits fully as a stranger (if acknowledged or bequeathed, she gets the lesser but without the cap of inheritance? Actually, the ruling is the same but the basis differs). The difference becomes critical when evaluating contradictory evidence: if suspicion is high, the iddah is computed from the time of acknowledgment to prevent collusion.
The fatwa of Bukhara/Samarkand scholars leans toward evaluating circumstances, not a rigid rule.
5
Resolve the apparent contradiction using fatwa
The commentator explains that if suspicion is evident, the iddah starts from the time of acknowledgment (to prevent collusion), and the rulings on zakat, testimony, and marriage are suspended. This harmonizes the two views: the ruling depends on the presence or absence of collusion indicators.
Thus, the mufta bihi position is to assess each case individually.

ثلاثاً ورثت وإن أبانها بأمرها في مرضه أو تصادقا عليها في الصحة ومضى العدة فاقرأ وقد طلقها ثلاثاً .انتهى. ولم يعلل بالرجعي وإنما علل بالواحدة وينبغي أن لا ميراث لها لرضاها بالبائن . قوله : (وإن أبانها بأمرها في مرضه أو تصادقا عليها في الصحة ومضى العدة فاقر أو أوصى لها فلها الأقل منها ومن ارثها أي لها الأقل من كل واحد من المقر به والموصى به ومن إرثها منه لأن العدة باقية في المسألة الأولى وهي سبب التهمة والحكم يدار على دليل التهمة، وفي الثانية قال الإمام ببقاء التهمة أيضاً لأن المرأة قد تختار الطلاق لينفتح باب الإقرار والوصية فيزيد حقها والزوجان قد يتواضعان على الإقرار بالفرقة وانقضاء العدة وهذه التهمة في الزيادة فرددناها ولا تهمة في قدر الميراث فصححناه وهما قالا في الثانية بنفي التهمة لكونها أجنبية لعدم العدة بدليل قبول شهادته لها وجواز وضع الزكاة فيها وتزوجها بزوج آخر. وأجاب الإمام الأعظم رضي الله عنه بأنه لا مواضعة عادة في حق الزكاة والشهادة والتزوج فلا تهمة. هذا حاصل ما في الهداية وقرره الشارحون من غير تعقب وهو ظاهر في أنه إذا أقر بالطلاق منذ زمان وصدقته أن العدة تعتبر من وقت الطلاق بدليل أنهم اتفقوا هنا أنه يجوز له دفع الزكاة إليها وشهادته لها وتزوجها وهو خلاف ما صرحوا به في العدة أن من الفتوى على أن العدة تعتبر من وقت الإقرار كما في الهداية والخانية وغيرهما، فلا يثبت شيء من هذه الأحكام ولا تزوجه بأختها وأربع سواها أيضاً فحينئذ ظهرت التهمة في إقراره أنه ووصيته واندفع به ما ذكره السروجي في غايته من ينبغي تحكيم الحال فإن كان جرى طلقني رجعياً فأبانها ا هـ قوله : ( وينبغي أن لا ميراث لها لرضاها بالبائن هذا هو الظاهر وهو مقتضى إطلاق المصنف بقوله سابقاً وإن أبانها بأمرها كما أشار إليه في النهر لكن ما في جامع الفصولين المذكور آنفاً يفيد أنها ترث لأنه علل بقوله إذ صار مبتدئاً أي أوقع شيئاً لم تطلبه فكأنه مسألة أوقع الثلاث ابتداء بدون طلب تأمل قوله : واندفع به ما ذكره السروجي الخ)
He said: 'If he divorced her thrice, she inherits; but if he made her irrevocable by her command in his illness, or they mutually agreed on it in health and the iddah passed, and he acknowledges or bequeaths to her, then she gets the lesser of the two: from what is acknowledged or bequeathed, and from her inheritance from him.' ... And he did not qualify with revocable but rather qualified with one (revocable) and it is appropriate that she should not inherit because she consented to the irrevocable.
