Conditional Divorce of a Slave Woman Freed Before Fulfillment
Ruling on conditional divorce of slave woman freed before fulfillment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce of a slave woman freed before the condition is fulfilled and identify the effect of manumission on the number of divorces.

Core Idea
Conditional divorces of a slave woman are reduced by one if she is freed before the condition occurs.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi divorce law where a husband issues a conditional triple divorce to his slave wife, but she is freed before the condition is met. The key question: does the triple pronouncement hold, or does manumission reduce the number? The source passage presents two narrations from later Hanafi texts that clarify the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and its components
Read the passage carefully. The husband says to his slave wife: 'If you enter the house, you are thrice divorced.' Then her master frees her, and she enters the house. Identify the key elements: the conditional pronouncement, the woman's status (slave at time of pronouncement), the manumission before the condition, and the fulfillment of the condition after manumission.
The condition is 'entering the house'; the pronouncement is 'thrice divorced'.
2
Determine the initial ruling before manumission
If the woman had remained a slave when the condition occurred, what would the ruling be? In Hanafi law, a conditional divorce on a slave wife: if the husband pronounces three divorces, only two divorces occur because a slave woman can only receive two divorces (the third is not effective as it would make her haram and she cannot be taken back). So without manumission, two divorces would occur.
The baseline: a slave woman's talaq count is limited to two.
3
Analyze the effect of manumission on the conditional divorce
Now, the woman is freed before the condition is fulfilled. Does she become like a free woman, so that all three divorces would now apply? The passage says no: only two divorces occur. Why? Because the conditional pronouncement was made while she was a slave, and the condition's fulfillment after manumission does not change the effect of the pronouncement. The intention and contract were based on her status at the time of the pronouncement.
The manumission does not retroactively change the nature of the pronouncement.
4
Compare the two narrations and identify the mufta bihi
Al-Qunyah states that two divorces occur. Jami' al-Karkhi adds that the husband also retains the right of revocable divorce (raj'a). The passage ends with a generalization that the ruling applies to all women (Muslim, kitabiyya, free, slave) due to the generality of proofs. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that two divorces occur and the husband may take her back during her 'iddah.
The presence of raj'a is important: the divorce is revocable, not final.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الرجعة طلقة واحدة وتمامه في الخانية في باب اللقيط . وفي القنية : قبيل النفقة : قال لزوجته الأمة إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثلاثاً ثم أعتقها مولاها فدخلت وقع ثنتان. وفي جامع الكرخي : طلقت ثنتين وملك الزوج الرجعة انتهى وأطلق في المرأة فشمل المسلمة والكتابية والحرة والمملوكة لإطلاق الدلائل كما في المحيط .
Chapter of Divorce, Section on Revocable Divorce. One divorce (from the previous discussion) and its completion in al-Khaniyyah in the chapter of foundlings. And in al-Qunyah: before the chapter of maintenance: he said to his slave wife: If you enter the house, you are thrice divorced, then her master freed her, then she entered, so two divorces occur. And in Jami' al-Karkhi: Two divorces occur and the husband has the right of revocable divorce. End. And he [the author] made it general regarding the woman, so it includes the Muslim woman, the Kitabiyya, the free woman, and the slave woman, due to the generality of the proofs as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a specific ruling about conditional divorce of a slave woman. The situation: a husband says to his slave wife, 'If you enter the house, you are thrice divorced.' Before she enters, the master frees her. Then she enters the house. The ruling: according to al-Qunyah, two divorces occur (not three), and according to Jami' al-Karkhi, two divorces and the husband still holds the right of revocable divorce (raj'a). The passage then generalizes: the word 'woman' in the text includes all types of women (Muslim, Kitabiyya, free, slave) because the proofs are general.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The revocable divorce where the husband may take back his wife during the waiting period without a new contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Two divorces
C) One divorce
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, if a slave woman is conditionally divorced thrice and freed before the condition is fulfilled, only two divorces occur and the husband retains the right of revocable divorce.

Pillars of Revocable Divorce: Verbal and Physical Acts
Detailed exposition of the pillars (verbal and physical) of raj'a with conditions and intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pillars of revocable divorce (raj'a) and apply the conditions for verbal and physical acts to determine whether a valid raj'a has occurred.

Core Idea
Raj'a can be established by explicit words without intention, but physical acts require desire and the woman's confirmation.

This lesson examines the pillars of revocable divorce (raj'a) as described in the Hanafi school. Understanding what constitutes a valid raj'a is crucial for the husband who wishes to resume the marriage during the 'iddah. The source presents both verbal and physical means, with detailed conditions and nuances that every student of fiqh must grasp.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of the pillar
Recognize that the pillar (rukn) of raj'a is either a word (qawl) or an action (fi'l).
This is the fundamental division; all subsequent details fall under one of these.
2
Distinguish between explicit and implicit verbal expressions
Explicit words (sarih) like 'I take you back' require no intention; implicit words (kinayah) like 'you are my wife' require intention (niyyah).
The text lists both types and their conditions.
3
Understand the ruling on using the word 'nikah' during 'iddah
Marrying her during 'iddah is considered a valid raj'a in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), contrary to some commentators' claims.
See the commentary on why this is the chosen view for fatwa.
4
Apply the conditions for physical acts
Any act that creates the prohibition of affinity (e.g., intercourse, kissing with desire) constitutes raj'a, provided desire is present and the woman confirms it if disputed.
The text lists examples and exceptions (e.g., without desire or looking at non-private parts).
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on intercourse after suspended divorce
Muhammad holds that remaining after intercourse makes it a raj'a; Abu Yusuf requires withdrawal. This affects the practical consequence.
The text does not give a tarjih; both views are recorded.

Source Text

وأنكره لا تثبت الرجعة وقدمنا في باب التعليق أنه لو قال لها إن جامعتك فأنت طالق فجامعها ومكث بعدما جامعها فهو رجعة عند محمد وقال أبو يوسف : لا يكون رجعة إلا أن يتنحى عنها، ولا تقبل الشهادة على فعلها لأن الشهوة لا تعرف إلا بقولها. وخرج ما إذا كانت هذه الأفعال بغير شهوة أو نظر إلى غير داخل الفرج بشهوة ولو إلى حلقة الدبر فإنه لا يكون مراجعاً لكنه مكروه كما في الولوالجية. وفي الجوهرة ولو صدقها الورثة بعد موته أنها لمسته بشهوة كان ذلك رجعة انتهى. وفي المعراج والأمة لو فعلت بالبائع في الخيار كان فسخاً لأن الفسخ قد يحصل بفعلها كما لو زنت أو قتلت نفسها، وأبو يوسف سوى بين الخيار والرجعة في أنهما لا يثبتان بفعلها ومحمد أثبت الرجعة دون الفسخ . وفي البدائع : الصحة
As for its pillar, it is a word or an action. The first (word) is explicit and implicit. As for the explicit: 'I take you back' and 'I take back my wife', and he combined both to cover if she is present so he addresses her, or absent. And 'I return you' and 'I reclaim you' and 'I retain you' and 'I hold you', so he becomes a retractor without intention. And from it (the explicit) is marriage and wedding; if he marries her during the waiting period, it is a revocable return in the apparent narration, as in al-Bada'i', and it is the chosen view as in al-Walwaljiyah, and the fatwa is upon it as in al-Yanabi'. So the statement of the commentators that it is not a revocable return according to Abu Hanifa contrary to Muhammad is not based on the apparent narration, as is not hidden, so it is known that the word 'marriage' is used metaphorically for revocable return. And is the word 'revocable return' used metaphorically for marriage? He said in al-Khulasa: If he divorces his wife then says: If I take you back, you are divorced, then when her waiting period expires, he marries her, she is not divorced. And if the divorce was irrevocable, she is divorced. And he explained it in al-Muhit as because she was not a place (for revocable return), it (the word) turned to marriage metaphorically. End. Its summary: if it is possible to turn the word to its true meaning at the time of suspension and it turned to it, it does not become metaphorical afterwards; otherwise it becomes metaphorical. As for the implicit (words), like 'you are with me as you were' or 'you are my wife', it depends on intention. As for the second, namely action, it indicates that every action that necessitates the prohibition of affinity is valid for revocable return. And he equated between word and action in order to avoid dislike, for it (the action) is disliked through action, as in al-Jawharah. So it includes intercourse, kissing with desire on any place, mouth, cheek, chin, forehead, head, touching without barrier or with a barrier that feels heat with it with desire, and looking into the interior of the private part with desire if she is reclining, and anal intercourse according to the fatwa because it is not free from touching with desire. And there is no difference between kissing, touching, and looking with desire from him or from her, provided she confirms him, whether it is with his permission or she did it stealthily, or he was sleeping, coerced, or insane. But if she claims it and he denies, the revocable return is not established. And we presented in the chapter of suspension that if he said to her: If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced, then he had intercourse with her and stayed after having intercourse, it is a revocable return according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It is not a revocable return unless he withdraws from her. And testimony is not accepted on her action because desire is only known by her statement. And excluded are cases where these actions were without desire, or looking at other than the inside of the private part with desire, even at the anal ring, then he is not a retractor, but it is disliked, as in al-Walwaljiyah. And in al-Jawharah: If the heirs confirm after his death that she touched him with desire, that is a revocable return. End. And in al-Mi'raj: If a slave woman does it to the seller during the option, it is a rescission because rescission can occur by her action, as if she commits adultery or kills herself. Abu Yusuf equated between option and revocable return in that both are not established by her action, while Muhammad affirmed revocable return but not rescission. And in al-Bada'i': Correctness.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion (sharh) on the pillars (ركن) of revocable divorce (الرجعة). It first divides the pillar into two types: verbal and physical. Verbal can be explicit (صريح) or implicit (كناية). The explicit words are listed and explained, including the important point that using the word 'nikah' (marriage) during the 'iddah is considered a valid raj'a in the apparent narration (ظاهر الرواية) contrary to some misunderstandings. The text then discusses whether the word 'raj'a' can be used metaphorically for a new marriage, deriving a rule from al-Khulasa. The physical pillar includes any action that creates the prohibition of affinity (حرمة المصاهرة), such as intercourse, kissing with desire, touching with desire, and looking with desire. The conditions for these actions to constitute raj'a are detailed, including the requirement of desire and the woman's confirmation. An intra-Hanafi difference is noted: Muhammad considers prolonged intercourse as raj'a automatically, while Abu Yusuf requires separation after intercourse. The passage concludes with a note on the option of the slave woman (خيار) and a cross-reference to al-Bada'i' for correctness.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The act of taking back one's wife during the revocable divorce waiting period without a new marriage contract
كناية (kinayah): An implicit verbal expression that indicates raj'a but requires intention (نية) to be effective

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man kisses his wife with desire during the revocable divorce waiting period, does that constitute a valid raj'a?
A) Yes, but only if he also says an explicit word
B) Yes, as long as she confirms he acted with desire
C) No, only verbal expressions count
D) No, because kissing is always without desire

Key Takeaway
Revocable divorce can be undone by explicit words without intention, or by physical acts with desire and confirmation, with nuances for implicit expressions and intra-Hanafi differences.

Talaq — Divorce
beginner
8 min
Verbal Expressions and Confirmation of Raj'a After 'Iddah
Ruling on post-'iddah claim of raj'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a husband's claim of raj'a after the 'iddah and identify when it is accepted or rejected based on the wife's confirmation.

Core Idea
The wife's confirmation decides the validity of a post-'iddah claim of raj'a.

When a husband claims he performed raj'a during the waiting period but the 'iddah has ended, his word alone is insufficient unless the wife confirms. This lesson parses the textual ruling from the passage, which treats two similar statements equally under Abu Hanifa's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A husband says 'I took you back' after the 'iddah has expired.
The claim is about a past act during the 'iddah.
2
Distinguish the two statements
The text gives two examples: the husband's claim 'I took you back during it' and the wife's response 'my 'iddah has passed' confirming or denying.
Both are treated symmetrically by Abu Hanifa.
3
Apply the rule of confirmation
If the wife confirms his claim, it is valid. If she denies and says her 'iddah has passed, her word is taken without oath.
The husband bears the burden of proof if she denies.
4
Note the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa equates the two statements in terms of evidentiary weight.
This is a ruling of tasdiq (confirmation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الرجعة براجعتك أو راجعت امرأتي وبما يوجب حرمة المصاهرة والإشهاد مندوب عليها ولو قال بعد العدة راجعتك فيها فصدقته تصح وإلا لا كراجعتك فقالت مجيبة مضت عدتي أبو حنيفة سوى بينهما في الثبوت. وفي شرح الطحاوي : لو قال أبطلت رجعتي أو لا رجعة لي عليك لا تبطل الرجعة انتهى. وفي القنية : أجاز مراجعة الفضولي صح ويصير مراجعاً بوقوع بصره على فرجها بشهوة من غير قصد المراجعة انتهى واختلف فيما إذا طلق رجعياً ثم جن ثم راجع بقول أو فعل فقيل لا يصح بهما، وقيل يصح بهما، وقيل تصح بالفعل دون القول كما في القنية من غير ترجيح، واقتصر البزازي على الأخير ولعله الراجح لما عرف أنه مؤاخذ بأفعاله دون أقواله وعلله في الصيرفية بأنه استدامة النكاح والرضا ليس بشرط ولهذا لو أكره على الرجعة بالفعل يصح انتهى. وفي الحاوي القدسي : وإذا راجعها بقبلة أو لمس فالأفضل أن يراجعها بالإشهاد ثانياً ا هـ . وفي المحيط : قال أبو يوسف: ويكره التقبيل واللمس بغير شهوة إذا لم يرد الرجعة ويكره أن يراها متجردة لأنه لا يأمن من أن يشتهي فيصير به مراجعاً ثم يحتاج إلى الطلاق فيؤدي إلى تطويل العدة انتهى.
Chapter of Divorce, Section on Revocable Divorce. By 'I take you back' or 'I take back my wife' and by what necessitates the prohibition of affinity, and witnessing is recommended upon it. And if he says after the waiting period: 'I took you back during it' and she confirms him, it is valid; otherwise not. Like 'I take you back' and she responds: 'My waiting period has passed.' Abu Hanifa equated between them in affirmation. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If he says: 'I annulled my revocable return' or 'I have no revocable return over you,' the revocable return is not annulled. End. And in al-Qunyah: He permitted the revocable return by a disinterested third party, valid, and he becomes a retractor by his sight falling on her private part with desire without intending the revocable return. End. And there is disagreement about when he divorces revocably, then becomes insane, then performs revocable return by word or action. Some said it is not valid by either; some said valid by both; some said valid by action not by word, as in al-Qunyah without preference. al-Bazzazi restricted to the latter, and perhaps it is the preferred view because it is known that he is held accountable for his actions, not his words. And he explained in al-Sayrafiyyah that it is continuation of the marriage, and consent is not a condition; hence if he is coerced into revocable return by action, it is valid. End. And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi: If he takes her back by a kiss or touch, it is better that he takes her back with witnessing a second time. End. And in al-Muhit: Abu Yusuf said: It is disliked to kiss and touch without desire if he does not intend the revocable return, and it is disliked to see her uncovered because he is not safe from desiring and thus becoming a retractor, then needing divorce, leading to lengthening of the waiting period. End.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage provides several subsidiary rulings (فروع) related to revocable divorce. First, it reiterates the verbal expressions that constitute raj'a. Then it discusses the case where a man claims to have performed raj'a during the 'iddah but after its expiry: his claim is accepted only if the woman confirms it, otherwise her denial is taken (without oath). This applies to both 'I take you back' and her claim 'my 'iddah has passed' — Abu Hanifa treats them equally. Next, it covers the ruling of annulling raj'a: merely saying 'I annul my raj'a' has no effect. Then, it permits a third party (فضولي) to perform the raj'a on behalf of the husband, and even a glance with desire can constitute raj'a without explicit intent. The passage then discusses a case where a man becomes insane after divorce but before performing raj'a: there is a difference of opinion on whether his subsequent raj'a by word or action is valid. The preferred view (as chosen by al-Bazzazi) is that action is valid but words are not, because actions follow intent and he is accountable for actions even if insane for words. Coercion into action is also valid. Finally, Abu Yusuf states it is disliked to kiss or touch without desire if not intending raj'a, and to see her uncovered to avoid involuntary desire.

Key Terms
العدة (al-iddah): The prescribed waiting period after divorce during which the woman cannot remarry
الرجعة (ar-raj'a): Revocable return to the marriage during the 'iddah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a husband claims he performed raj'a during the 'iddah after it has expired, and the wife denies?
A) The claim is accepted if the husband swears an oath
B) The claim is rejected because the wife denies
C) The claim is accepted only if two witnesses saw the raj'a
D) The claim is accepted regardless of the wife's response

Key Takeaway
After the 'iddah, a husband's claim of raj'a is valid only if the wife confirms it.

intermediate

Third-Party Raj'a and Involuntary Acts: Fuduli, Glance, and Coercion
Rulings on third-party and involuntary raj'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that a third party can perform raj'a on behalf of the husband, and that a glance with desire or coerced action also constitutes valid raj'a.

Core Idea
Raj'a can be effected by a third party, a desirable glance, or even under coercion, because intent is not a strict condition.

The passage expands the scope of actions that constitute raj'a beyond explicit verbal consent. It validates raj'a performed by an unauthorized third party (fuduli), a mere glance with desire without explicit intention, and even an act done under coercion. This lesson unpacks these subsidiary rulings and their underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling: third-party raj'a
The passage from al-Qunyah states that if a disinterested third party (fuduli) performs the raj'a, it is valid and takes effect.
This is a permission for someone else to act without prior authorization.
2
Analyze the glance ruling
Looking at the wife's private part with desire, even without intending raj'a, makes him a retractor (muraji').
This shows that desire itself can trigger the legal consequence.
3
Examine the coercion ruling
If a man is coerced into performing an act of raj'a, it is valid because consent is not a requirement.
The text explicitly states 'consent is not a condition.'
4
Understand the rationale
Raj'a is seen as a continuation of the marriage (istidamat al-nikah), which does not require the husband's express consent for every act.
This principle allows for these broader validations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الرجعة براجعتك أو راجعت امرأتي وبما يوجب حرمة المصاهرة والإشهاد مندوب عليها ولو قال بعد العدة راجعتك فيها فصدقته تصح وإلا لا كراجعتك فقالت مجيبة مضت عدتي أبو حنيفة سوى بينهما في الثبوت. وفي شرح الطحاوي : لو قال أبطلت رجعتي أو لا رجعة لي عليك لا تبطل الرجعة انتهى. وفي القنية : أجاز مراجعة الفضولي صح ويصير مراجعاً بوقوع بصره على فرجها بشهوة من غير قصد المراجعة انتهى واختلف فيما إذا طلق رجعياً ثم جن ثم راجع بقول أو فعل فقيل لا يصح بهما، وقيل يصح بهما، وقيل تصح بالفعل دون القول كما في القنية من غير ترجيح، واقتصر البزازي على الأخير ولعله الراجح لما عرف أنه مؤاخذ بأفعاله دون أقواله وعلله في الصيرفية بأنه استدامة النكاح والرضا ليس بشرط ولهذا لو أكره على الرجعة بالفعل يصح انتهى. وفي الحاوي القدسي : وإذا راجعها بقبلة أو لمس فالأفضل أن يراجعها بالإشهاد ثانياً ا هـ . وفي المحيط : قال أبو يوسف: ويكره التقبيل واللمس بغير شهوة إذا لم يرد الرجعة ويكره أن يراها متجردة لأنه لا يأمن من أن يشتهي فيصير به مراجعاً ثم يحتاج إلى الطلاق فيؤدي إلى تطويل العدة انتهى.

Key Terms
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized third party who performs a legal act on behalf of another without prior permission
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compulsion or coercion that overrides free will

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid way to effect raj'a according to the passage?
A) A glance with desire without intention
B) A coerced verbal pronouncement by the husband
C) A third-party statement without authorization
D) A mere thought with desire without any action or glance

Key Takeaway
Raj'a can be validly effected by a third party, a desirable glance, or even under coercion, because the marriage is continuous and consent is not strictly required.
